Of the extraction of the secondine.
When the secondine is retained in childbirth, then you command the patient to retain her breath, and then make that stir with a condition, and strengthen your hand over the mouth of her and over her nostrils, and if by this it is expelled outside, it is good.
And if not, then you take a cloth and perforate in the cover a hole and place in that herbs burning the mouth of the mother, as such as calamint and rue and anise and chamomile and sicadas arabic and cassia lignea and santaurea. All these herbs or some of them are submerged in water in the cloth, and place that over the fire.
And then place a tube of cane in the mouth of the mother, and keep that inside so that the vapor comes to the cavity of the mother.
And then make that stir as we have said, and the secondine is ejected outside with velocity.
But if remaining after this cure and it is not expelled, then you command the midwife to submerge her left hand in sesame oil or in a mixture of altea, and then introduce that into the vagina and search with that the secondine, and as she has found it, then take it and extend it little by little. But if it is annexed in the perineum of the mother, then introduce her hand as we have recounted, and when she has found the secondine, extract it little by little according to rectum so that the mother does not feel the traction vehement. It is necessary that it be changed to the right and left sides with facility.
And if the mouth of the mother is constricted, we have already recounted the cure with stir and with medication of the cloth with herbs.
But if it is not expelled with all the causes which we have recounted, then guard that you do not tire on that in iteration of extraction, but rather that what is emitted from it to the mouth of the mother lies on the cloth of the woman, and then christenize that with an enguent and tetrafarmacon; for that will make it rot so that after a few days and it will be dissolved and it will be expelled.
But when you may, it is raised bad odor to the head, and to the stomach, and what you read the patient; and then it is necessary that fumigations and conveniences be administered to it.
But some of the Ancients are already explored fumigation of nazitort and dried figs.
The form of instrument with which the woman is fumigated for the retention of menstruation and the secondine:
It is made of  wood or of bronze.
And it is placed the fine extremity in the vagina and the wide extremity over the fire, and the fumigation is placed over the embers of the fire.
And keep that inside so that the fumigation is consumed and iterate another.
Of the cure of the unperforated anus of the child.
Many children are born and their anuses are not perforated, which already have a subtle and opaque. Therefore it is necessary for the midwife to pierce with her finger that opaque; and if not, pierce it with a sharp spatula, and guard that it does not touch the sphincter.
And then place on that wool soaked in wine and oil; and then cure that with an enguent until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is opaque, then place in the hole a tube for several days, and remove it when the child will want to defecate.
And already accidents opacity of the anus by consolidation of plague or of abscess; therefore it is necessary that that consolidation be incised, and then it be cured with what we have said of the cure and of the lead.
Of the cure of fistulas that end below.
The fistulas that develop in the lower part below the navel and their thickness which develop near the anus or in the space of one part.
And it is a fistula or a tumor.
And when that lump is old and runs from the white, moist, or subtle pus, and sometimes from these fistulas, some penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by what is emitted from them by the exit and by the windiness; and sometimes the patient feels the exit, and very often it is emitted from them worms.
And sometimes from these fistulas, some penetrate into the space into the bladder, and into the meat of the vagina; and sometimes from these penetrating into the junction of the thigh and into the knot of the hip.
And in which it is known that a fistula penetrates into the anus and does not penetrate, is that you put your finger inside the anus, and you insert a subtle probe of lead or iron into the fistula when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity, then you insert a subtle probe of lead or horsehair into it until you feel the probe or hair in your finger.
And if you do not feel it at all, nor do you process any cause of ejection or worms or windiness, as we have said, then you know that it is non-penetrating, and then you turn to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the vagina, then the meaning of it is the emission of urine from that and prohibition of it so that the place with medicine may be consolidated.
But if it is penetrating into the junction of the thigh or into the bone and causing pain in the circuit, then the sign of it is that the probe penetrates here if there is no tortuosity into the bone, and causing pain in the circuit of the part; and that from it there is pus always; and that it does not value in that medication, nor is it consolidated with an enema.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no cure of all, and the cure of those is the work and vanity of the medical leaves upon which they are founded.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing with iron is expected according to what I will tell you, and what I have already done and proved.
And it is that the patient lies between your hands on his two and elevates his legs up, the thighs being inclined to the belly of him, and then insert a probe of lead, or of lead if there is tortuosity in the fistula, until you know the place where the probe penetrates.
And if that feels the patient around the buttock, then cover that you insert your finger inside the buttock.
And if you feel in your finger the probe already by itself penetrating, or another thing that you feel between your finger and that siphac or flesh, then you know certainly that it is penetrating; and therefore do not work on that, for it is not in that healing, as we have said.
But however, some say that it is healed in some men tart.
And of the medication by which healing is expected in it is that you ignite a subtle cautery according to the width of the fistula, as it is preceded, and insert that ignited one into the fistula until it reaches around the buttock; and then repeat that two or three times until you know that all those flesh adjusted to the fistula have been cauterized, which are like the quill of a bird's feather.
And then cure that with poultices wrapped in border until they can all those flesh that are cauterized, and then cure that with medicines the flesh generating and consolidating, and if it is healed, well is; and if not, then it is not healed in any time by other cure.
And if the probe enters, and does not penetrate into your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe which is veiled with flesh or skin, and sometimes the fistula in that which seizes the surface of the codena, then dry the codena from the beginning of the fistula, and you see drying with the probe, and in the fistula until the drying reaches the place where the extremity of the probe penetrates, and by the probe is liberated and caught.
And then purify those fistula flesh which are assembled to the quill of a bird's feather and do not break from those any cause.
But if flow of blood sometimes comes between you and your operation when you were inserting those flesh, then the best that may be and of the lighter pus instrument is to cauterize with fire or with acute medicine, because cauterizing with fire adjusts good two habits: it is the cauterization of those flesh adjusted, and abscess of the blood excretion of the humidities.
And then cure that with poultices wrapped in border or in caus trissat until the place gets brac, and then cure the plague with enemas flesh hard generating and consolidating.
And so medicines in which there is styptic and excretion; when then it is restored it is already healed and you do not fear iteration.

And therefore if you feel the probe and between you and it is veiled with skin or flesh, and is proper to the penetration, then there is no operation in it but according to my trust and of hope.

Of suture of hemorrhoids in which blood runs in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are in two ways, because they are between the buttock seeming like that of carbons, or those that are like the fat of rasim. And of those so small and large, and that the blood runs from those always and is one, and are many, and are outside the buttock, and in the extremities of it.
But however those that are outside the buttock are according to the less of moisture, of those that run clear water or a little blood with a continuous body, and are according to the color of the body.
And the cure of that which is inside the buttock is that you command the patient to lie down and to express until the buttock is empty and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you take and suspend with hooks or what you have those with your nail, and then you cut those of their roots.
But if the hooks are not held in those because of the moisture of them and their softening, then take those with rough cloth or with a stick or with a fleece of wool, and extract those with your fingers, and then cut those and pulverize over them some powders caudas near the incision, so that they are applied to those in place of cauterization; or cauterize those according to what is preceded in the chapter of cauterization. And then cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
And if the buttock does not obey the egress, then cristerize the patient with a crister in which there is a small bite, and wash it with that water that is in the buttock and that obeys the egress slightly when the patient is expressed.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the buttock, then the cause of them is lighter, and it is that you take those with your nail, or that you take those with a hook, and cut those, and then cure those with the water that we have said until they are healed.
And that which you have incised, cover that you administer suture of them according to this way: take feel enveloped and introduce that into a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids to the parts above, and make that penetrate with the needle in the root of the other part, and envelop the two extremities of the feel in the part below the needle, and so laterally; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong structure, and then untie the feel and extract the needle.
And do this to all, and leave one of them untied, so that the superfluity of blood runs from that.
And then put on the buttock cloth infused in rose oil or in cero; and put that on the buttock and command the patient to rest. And then leave that until it dries; and when it is dried, cure those with ointments or with other cure until it is healed.
And because old ones are not helpful in this medicine, then when you see those with a point of a spatula or with your fingernail, while they are moist and have a host of pus from them, which prevents them from consolidating; and then cure them when they are consolidated.
Then you must scrape those with vigorous pus, the first ones that have blood and are excoriated; and then cure them when they are healed.
Know that hemorrhoids, it is key, and it is not if not a red nodule according to the color of the body resembling a head of a key, and it is made in all the body and mostly in the parts below the feet and in the buttocks, and therefore by those pains you must know the ailment.
And therefore, when you see that which is in the circuit of the key, and it is held with the forceps or with an instrument, and it is eradicated from its root; and then cure that.
And if you wish, cauterize as previously described in its chapter.
But warts are a small, rough, round fistula on the surface of the body, and many times they come in the most of the children.
And the cure of them is that you extend and cut that, or that you tighten that with a thread or with a needle while it is being cauterized. And if you wish, cauterize that, as we have said, with fire or with an acute medicine.
But a nettle is a small, rough, or thick fistula on the surface of the body, going into the skin, and when the sting touches that, it is felt in it, resembling a bite of a wasp, and it is made around the body.
And the cure of it is that you take a quill of eagle or of vulture, and it is strong, and place that over the nettle while you contain it with the circle of the quill from the parts of it; and then you revolve your hand with the quill while you cut that which is in the circuit of the nettle, and you place that in the skin of the flesh, and that you eradicate the nettle from its root.
And if you wish, do that with a quill of iron or of steel, according to this form:
Let the upper part of the quill be drawn to a subtlety, blunt (so it is not perforated), enveloped so that over the said, the apprehension of it be abolished and the revolution of that.
And if you wish, cauterize the nettle according to what is previously said in the cauterization of a boil in the chapter of cauterization.
On the form of the instruments that are administered to give baptism.
Sometimes baptism is performed with silver or with lead or with iron melted or hammered; and sometimes it is made of those instruments small and large according to those to whom it is administered, that is, that which is administered in the cure of the little children be small; and of those whose necks are weak or sore, the baptisms of those are small.
The form of a large and few baptism:

And the extremity of it further down, which is inserted into the anus, be blunt (so it is not open) much like a lion, drawn to a subtlety; and on one side of it there are two holes, and on the other side one hole, and be the width of the hole according to the thickness of the rays and a little thicker.
And be the bag in which are placed the medicines of animal bladder or of sheepskin, and the root of it be a palm.
And be it perforated by many holes as it is revolved; and if the space which is between the holes the thickness of the finger.
And then insert into those holes a strong thread of strong thread, the one of ten threads, or about those; and adjust with that the bag; and when it is placed in it the bag, be straight in the head of the baptismal font between two distinguishing ones with strong constriction, and then cauterize with that each hole: But the quality of cauterizing with that is that you fill the bag with the medicine, that it be heated according to the quantity of the tolerance of the patient; and guard yourself that it be not too hot nor too cold.
And then the patient be laid on his back, and elevate his legs and place under him a cushion on which already are the men or a pillow; and then anoint the extremity of the cautery with oil or with egg white or with a mucilage of fennel or of similar; and then insert that into the anus with facility, and then the minister takes the bag and expresses that with two hands and at the same time with vehemence until it be evacuated in the bowel all that which is in the baptismal font; and then extract the baptismal font and the patient raise his legs to the wall.
And if the medicine forces that to emission, hold as much as it may; and if it sleeps with that for a whole night, it is more beneficial in healing.
But if you are treating those with diseases of the solution of the belly and the expulsion of it the loin, then you iterate the cautery until it is healed.
And if in the anus there are hemorrhoids or boils, then be the cautery very subtle and lion, of the cauteries with which the children are cauterized.
Of the cure of the plagues.
And I have already said in the division of the cure of the plagues what was convenient to the free, and I say thus the cure of those with my and with medicines according to the complement.
Therefore I say that the plagues are diversified according to what in which the plagues are made and according to the place on which the plagues fall.
And the causes for which many times the plagues are made are such as a wound from a stone, or incision from a sword or dagger, or percussion with a lance, or with a club, or with an arrow, or similar to those of many causes.
But the plagues that are in the parts of the body are such as a plague that falls on the head, or the neck, or the feet, or the belly, or the arm, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I am recounting the cure of some plagues so that you pause that reasoning and rule over the other plagues.
And I will begin with the plagues of the head simple, for the remembrance of the composed will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when a simple plague happens in the head and it is not a fracture, then consider: if it is from a wound from a stone or similar, and the wound has already been cleaned only and it is a large plague and you fear about that patient having an abscess, then you cauterize and flebotomize the cephalic immediately, and do not delay this; and extract to that of the blood according to the quantity of the virtue of it if no other cause prohibits fleubotomia, more especially if the blood that is drawn from the plague is little.
And be the fleubotomia of the opposite side of the afflicted part; and guard the patient from repletion and from wine and from heavy foods.
But if from the ministration any cause prohibits, then be it lessened of the food and do not approach to meat nor to wine.
And pause on the plague, if to it there happens an abscess cauterize, coat infused in rose oil or in wine in which it is steeped.
But if it is sure of an abscess cauterize, then pause on the plague, if it is recent with its blood and the air has not changed that the powder which I will recount aprop small.
And if the air has already changed that, then pause on poultices until it is healed.
But if the plague is great or is from an incision from a sword and similar, and the lips of it are not adjusted by puss, then adjust those with suture according to that I will recount of the suture of them.
But if the plague has already been discovered from the bone and skin, and there is no retention of it but a small suspension, then incise that and then cure the plague with medicines and restore the flesh in the place of the wound, and command the patient to be sparing of foods in which there is thickness: caps and forms cooked and similar to them, until it is composed.
And if there is a putrefaction in the wound and it does not adhere to the bone, then you incise all that putrefied and throw it away, and then cure that.
But if in these plagues there is an artery or vein from which the flow of blood could come, and it is not incised by medicines, then you inquire about the artery, and if you find that not incised, then you incise that with a spatula, or tie that; and if necessity provokes that what we have said does not confer, then you cauterize until the blood is stopped.
And if these plagues are simple and small, then the cause of them is lighter; inasmuch that you pulverize on those this powder, for consolidated that before the air changes them.
And the manner of the powder is this: receive olive oil parted one, of dragon's blood parted two, of cauterized no-extinct parted three; be all three ground and sifted, and of it be the plague filled, and be constricted by good constriction until well over that it adheres, and be coagulated with the blood.
And after strengthens that with poultices and leave it, and do not untie it while it is so that it is not suppurated, for no medicine is eradicated from it until it is healed.
And sometimes do that alone cauterize, when it is not to you in present olive oil and dragon's blood.
And already do this same operation in the great plagues when it is convenient that it be paused on those and be tightened, and are recent plagues with their blood, those which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and more especially when it is in summer time, then it is not understood in the position of this powder, but rather cure those with what we have said, inasmuch that you pause on those poultices digested, or that you pause on those powder of barley flour with water and honey until they get putrefaction; and then cure those with the other cure until they are healed.
But if a fracture occurs in the bone, and it is small, then you extract it with a splint.
And I will say the cure for head fractures in those that are venomous.
Of the plagues of the neck.
But if the fracture occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in treatment, and between the fracture of the head when it is simple.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut or artery, then if it is a nerve, it is not in its place, but that its cure is avoided with that which makes vehement clotting, such as zinc and zeger that is vitriol and similar to their wounds to the nerves and counteracts that. Therefore I say that when the wound is small and there is some cause of the hernia through that, and it is made difficult to reduce that, then it is done in one of two ways: or by the smallness of the incision as we have said, or when the hernia has come inflammation because of the cold of the air.

And let the needles be median between thickness and fineness, because the fine needles lightly incised the flesh; and the thick ones are of difficult entrance in the cord, and therefore for that reason let them be median in fineness and thickness.

Of the cure of  zoster and fistula.
Know that every plague and abscess when it is inflamed and becomes ancient, and is made an ulcer, and is not consolidated and sweats all the time and is not cut off, then, in whatever member it may be, universally it is called a fistula, and we call it that zoster.
And fistula according to truth is a fistula, hard, white with which there is no pain and to which the cavity is as the cavity of a bird's bone; and for this reason, it is called by some "rizatz," that is, bone.
And it is in some people humid, which sweats; and very soon the humidity is cut off in some hours. And sometimes this humidity is much, and is little, and is thick, and is fine.
I have shown all those causes, the signs of them, and their cure with medicines in the division.
And I tell you as I have done with the cure of fistula, which is made by way of operation with my hand.
And know that fistula occurs in all parts of the body; for some of them reach the great veins and arteries, or the nerves, or the head, or the intestine, or the bladder, or any of the ribs, or any of the vertebrae of the spine, or any of the joints composed in such a way that they are connected by the hand and the foot; and of these, those that give rise to them in proximity to the main limb and its likeness.

And if the fistula is having many horrible things, it is not possible for you to signify upon it with a probe.

Of the cure of the bowels and the quality of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it makes a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and pus is extracted from it, and the place remains empty as if it were a vessel, and the skin that is above it as if it were fine cloth, and it has not reached the back corruption, and it has not imprinted on the bone or on the nerves or on the ligament, it is called a sinus, and it is not called a fistula.
But however, if the space is prolonged until the corruption presses on some member, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the cure of it is that the skin is incised entirely as if it were rolled up, and more so when it is fine and made like cloth, and composes that it does not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin adheres and that that corruption has not achieved further consequence, and in the skin there has passed through from the front, then you cure that with clysters.
And it is that you consider the sinus, and then if it is large, and the brac that you see is of fetid odor, then you clysterize that with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and strong wine and ginger receiving equal parts, and they are placed in a vessel, and they are boiled over the fire until the medicine becomes thick and reaches the consistency of honey.
And then you take from it what is necessary and dissolve it with water and honey; and clysterize with that the sinus, and strengthen the orifice of it, and leave the medicine in it for the quantity of two hours.
And then you extract that with a sponge; and do this for so many days until the sinus is purified and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with water of ash, when the medicine is not present to you; and it is that you take the ash of wheat, and put water over it; and then collect that and clysterize with it until you confess that the sinus is then healed.
And if the patient does not have tolerance over the Egyptian medicine nor the water of ash, then clysterize that with water and honey and that it be mixed with ginger, and clysterize that with wine and honey mixed, because of the property of honey is that it purifies and washes, and the wine makes the sinus adhere, and more so when in the wine there is superfluity of astringency and dryness.
And as you do this sometimes and you confess that the sinus is already healed, then clysterize that with water that makes flesh grow in it.

And similarly if the orifice is wide, then you add the lips of it with suture and leave it for the quantity in which between the clyster its addition.
And if the patient's orifice from which the arm protrudes is elevated to the upper parts, then dry that in the lowest place that is in it so that the arm runs into it, because when the arm is contracted in the pre-existence of the sinus, the flesh is generated in it.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus into the environment of the lowest bone as you want, then pierce it so that you prepare the member with the preparation by which the arm runs lightly according to what is at your apparel and that no cause of the arm is retained in it.

And if from the sinus after many days is issued undigested arm, then you know that the sinus does not adhere; and if the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and is the prolonged cause, then you adjust in drying of the medicines, and if the medicines are humid in their substance and in their virtue, they are dried as a diaphani conguent, when in it is adjusted superfluity of addition of zegi.

Of the cure of panariti and ingrown toenails and the addition of a finger incision and section.
Panariti is an abscess that arises from under the nail of the finger of the hand or foot, and very often arises in the other fingers, and as it is prolonged and its cure is less valued, or it is an abscess of an abscess caut, or it is corrupted, and throws out arm while it corrodes the roots of the nail, and very often corrupts the whole, and very often spreads its corruption to the bone while it is to it a fetid odor, and is made the extremity of the finger lamed, and the color of it is made dark.
And therefore when you will have that with the water that we have said in the division and do not confer your cure, then cove that you incise with iron all the superfluity that is remaining from the nail and then cauterize the wound with fire, because fire cauterization is useful in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail voids already compress the flesh to the sides of the finger, and they begin to prick it and let it out, then cove that you place a fine ray under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and impel it upwards and incise it with ease, and place on that which remains of the flesh of the medicinal corrosive concoctions until it is all removed; and then cure it with enguent until it is healed.
But if the corruption has already entered the bone, then cove that you incise the bone and extract it, because no time is healed that in it the bone is corrupted.
And if you see the corrosion and corruption going into the wound, then you cut it in one of the nuts according to how it is pressed its memory, and then take care of it while it is healing.
But if the nail is bruised or rubbed, and there is pain in that area, then when you first begin to treat the patient, and then dry the nail with a sharp spatula, making a transverse section from top to bottom, and guard yourself that the section does not reach into the flesh that is left from the nail, for you will cause the patient great pain, and it will be the cause of the birth of superfluous flesh in the area; and then take care of that area with water that relieves pain.
But this adjusted flesh, which is born in the hands of some men, it is very hard, all flesh, and very hard in the bones, or very hard in the nail.
And some of them are born at the root of the joint, or the birth of them in some nuts of the hands. But that one that is born in some nuts of the finger does not get worse, and this one that is born in the joints very hard is worse.
And of those that are born fleshy, the section is lighter, and it is that you cut that according to its root with a broad spatula.
But of that one that is born at the root of the joint, the healing is difficult; and therefore guard yourself from the abscess of it.
But in that one that is born in the finger, when you first cut the flesh of it with a round incision into the bone, and then saw it with one of those saws convenient to water, and then take care of it while it is healing.
But consolidation, which happens to the fingers between their many movements or by birth of the man who is born with it, or by consolidation of plague or by burning of fire, or by similarities, then when you cut that consolidation while the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between their cloths of the bandages in olive oil rubbed so that they are not consolidated lightly, and separate between them, or place between them a lamina of subtle lead while it is consolidating according to what is necessary.

Of incisions of vices, they are varicose.
Vices are varicose, and they are veins convoluted thick and full of melancholic superfluities which come in motion in the limbs of the body, and the major advent of them in the legs, and more in the legs of the travelers and the laborers and the carriers.
Therefore, it is necessary that you administer purging of the body of black bile some times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilica vein.
But the cure of them with iron is according to two manners: one is that it be incised and that the black blood be thrown out, and the other manner is that the vein be elevated and all of it be extracted.
The incision of it is according to this manner: first embroil the leg with lukewarm water so that the thick blood be resolved, which is feculent of the leg, and then strengthen the leg of the patient to the most sovereign part of the thigh, and to the part below the knee with a bandage, and then cut the vein in one place, or in two, or in three with a wide incision, and then elevate the black blood with your hand from the part below the leg and upwards, and downwards, until the quantity of blood that you want and that you see is sufficient, and which will take the virtue of the ailment.
And then apply that, and command him to abstain from foods that engender black bile.
And repeat the cure by phlebotomy when the veins are full and that night to the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this manner: you that raze the leg of the patient, if in it there are movements, and then put it in a bath and embroil the leg of it with lukewarm water until it becomes reddish and the veins are exuberant; or that it be exercised by strong exercise, if the bath is not present, until the limb be scalded.
And then cut the vein in the right of the vein by incision in the long or in the back of it below the knee, or in the lowest part of it below the ankle.
And then open the vein with forceps and scrape the vein from all parts until it appears to the touch, and it is of the appearance of it that sometimes that rogue is declining to blackness.
And therefore when you evacuate and from the vein you see that white which is cord.
And then you put below that the ray until it be elevated and be thrown from the vein, and suspend that with a lance or lion's horn.
And then cut another incision near that incision for three fingers, and then scrape the vein from the part below the vein until it appears.
And then elevate that and cut it in the back incision below the ankle, and then extract that and elevate it until it is drawn out by the second incision, and then extract that from the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract that from the incision that is most sovereign or most high of all the incisions, until all is drawn out; and then cut that.
And if it does not obey to you to extract and elevate, then introduce a needle with a strong double thread and tie that and extract it, or introduce behind it the ray and turn your hand to all parts within which it is thrown. And guard yourself that it not be cut; for if it is broken, it is made difficult for you to elevate that gut and it comes upon the patient for that harm.
And when that whole gut is put back, then place upon the place of the plague wool soaked in wine and oil rubbed and oil unrubbed, and cure that until it is healed.
And if the gut is contracted having tortuosity in which it is wrapped in the parts, and it is not according to rectitude according to what we have said, then cover that which you do upon that whole part of tortuosity of it and to the places of its wrapping, and then suspend that with hooks until you elevate that whole.
And guard yourself that your section that you do not cut the vein and that you do not wound that, for difficult it will be for you to elevate that; and serve your prudence in that.
Form of the incision with which the gut is cut:
It is not to it sharpened as with the hooks nor is it of sharp extremity so that it does not wound the vein; and it is of gross flexibility leon, for if it were subtle, it would cut the vein by its subtlety; having in it superfluous thickness as we have said.

On the section for the ailment of nafir.
This illness is called nafir in our land, and it is pain which comes in certain members, and then it is changed from member to member.
And I have already seen that, as I will tell you. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflation in the vein of the arm, and since it was warm for an hour, I saw that inflation go as if it were a worm rising, but more lightly than being by, as if it were living silver when it runs from place to place.
And then the pain went to that place and it spread to the muscle, and then it stayed there for an hour.
And then it corrected in the other body until it reached the other arm.
And then I recommended to her that she should roll herself over her whole body as I had seen; and I was too amazed by the change of it and the speed of member to member. And I had already seen that before in my own heels in that manner, however, I have seen many who attribute pain to be changed from member to member. But I did not see that in the heels as in the woman.
And I did not think that it was anything but that because the woman was old, on so of dry body and of exposed veins, and in this appearance it felt that changeable windiness.
And it is necessary that it does not appear according to this retrograde cure in those who are delicate and with humid bodies of well-covered veins.
And as you want the cure of it, and the patient feels that pain, then it is not to you apparent at the temple as we have said, then you tighten over that and leave it slightly and squeeze over that until that windiness is constricted, and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it at your temples, then you cure that with the shaking of the body and with that which you expel windiness, and charms those with foul pills and with pills of serapi and the like.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to the species, and according to the places of the body in which they strike, for their diversity is that of those that have large points, and small, and concave, and blunt, some are solid; and of those that have three angles, and of those that have tongues; and of those that have shafts.
And of the signs of the servant is when it strikes in it an arrow, and pierces the bone, and it is felt from the cyphasis which is above the brain, for it causes by it soda vehement, and scotomia, and vertigo, and roaring in the heels, and inflammation and roaring of the tongue, and spasm and mixture of understanding and vomiting of bile, and very soon is cut off the word and destroyed the voice, and it is felt from the place of the plague a white humidity resembling powder, and eyes of it resembling the fluidity of the flesh.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then you abstain from the cure and the extraction of the arrow, if it is not outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it strikes in the heart, are that it is near the left nipple, and felt with that which it has been placed in hard cause, and it is not a void cause; and very soon the movement of the arrow is similar to the movement of the hand, and running of the plague black blood, and it feels that coldness of the extremities, and sweat cold, and syncope; and therefore know that death descends all the way down.
And the signs when the arrow has plagued the lung, are so easily blood spume from the plague, and the vessels that are near the neck are apoplectic, and the color of the patient is changed, and he expires great spirit, and demands the attraction of cold air.
And if it strikes the arrow in the ligament which is in the feet, and the pain then is near the small ribs, and it is made the leg great with vehement pain and with sigh, and not are bad all the members of the muscles.
And if it strikes the arrow in the stomach, then very soon eyes of the plague food any undigested.
And if it strikes the arrow in the belly, and it is placed in it, eyes some wound of the plague, or the membrane, or the intestine which is dislocated, and there is no hope in the cure of it extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow enters the bladder, and urine is expelled outside, and some process occurs from it to the external parts, and there is a great deal of pain on the patient, then you know that it is mortal.
May the other members, as well as the face, neck, throat, shoulders and arms, and the vertebrae of the back, and the clavicle, and the hip, and the leg and their likenesses, be saved according to the major dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and is not a venomous arrow.
And I recount to you what I have seen of the nature of these arrows, so that you may signify it to you about your cure.
The cause is, that in a man a shot arrow in the lacrimal of his eye at the root of the nose, and you extracted that from the other part, leaving the gray of the eyebrow of him, and he is healed, and it did not harm in the eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew who was shot in the gray of his eye, leaving the eyelid, and the arrow was already hidden and could not be obtained from it except for a small extremity with which it adhered to its shaft, and it was a large arrow of the sharp arrows of the Turks, with a lion's head in which there were no feathers.
And the Jew healed, and it did not harm in his eye at all.
And I extracted another arrow from a Christian man who was shot in the throat, and it was an Arabic arrow, and that is the one that has feathers; and therefore I incised it between two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat; and therefore I subtly maneuvered it, and then extracted it, and the Christian healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who was shot in his belly, and I thought he would die from it; and therefore, because forty days passed and there was no change in him from his dispositions, I incised on the arrow and maneuvered it, and he healed, and it did not harm him at all.
And I saw another man in whom an arrow was shot in his back, and the wound was consolidated on him, and because it was about seven years, he pulled the arrow out at the root of the groin.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow was shot in her belly, and the wound was consolidated from within her, and she was not changed in her dispositions in any way, and the woman did not suffer any harm in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow was shot in his face and the wound was consolidated, and he broke the arrow, not feeling much pain, and many appearances were like this.
And I extracted the arrow from the man of the king's princes who was shot in the middle of his nose and had already declined to the right side a little, and the whole arrow was hidden.
And I was called to his cure about three days after the shooting of the arrow.
And then I felt the wound of the arrow very strictly; and then I inquired about it with subtle attempts, and I did not feel it; but I felt a prick below the right feather, and therefore I suspected that prick was because of the extremity of the arrow.
And I plastered the place with a plaster in which there is attractive and digestive virtue, confessing to myself that the place will be festered so that it appears to me the place of the arrow, so that it will dry on it.
And it did not harm in the place significantly that the arrow had reached that place; and I continued with the plaster on it for many days and it did not harm at all; and the wound was consolidated meanwhile, and he broke the wound from the extraction of it for a space of many days until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose and recounted this to me.
And then I placed on the wound medicine acute corrosive for many days until it opened, and with the attempt I felt it, and I felt the extremity of the arrow subtly, which adhered to the shaft.
And then I adjusted it in the appearance of the wound with that acute medicine until it appeared to me visibly the extremity of the arrow, and it passed to me time with it for the space of time within four months.
And then because the wound had been amplified, and it was possible for me to intervene with forceps in it, and remove it, without any impediment, then I did not cease to subtly maneuver and engineer on it with manners of ingenuity, until one day I began on it with manners of ingenious to hang it with forceps carefully made, according to how it will come in the end of the chapter, until I extracted it; and then I restored that wound.

Of phlebotomy of the veins.

Of suction and the qualities of using them.
Suctions are of horns, or of glass, or of wood.
And suction is done in two ways: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is suction without scarification.
And this suction that is without scarification is in two ways, whether it is with fire or without fire.
And to the suctions that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood there are fourteen locations of the body, of which one in which suctions are placed is the occiput, and it is the back part of the head; and the middle location of the two parts of the neck together; and suctions of the chin below the jawbone below the mouth; and suctions of the two shoulder blades; and suctions of the hypochondrium, and it is above the end of the rib cage; and suctions of the two armpits; and suctions of the two groins.
And suction does not extract the blood but from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for this reason it does not capture the virtue through them, as it is done by phlebotomy; nor is it advisable that suction be done with fire or without fire in any illness caused by repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity provokes suction for illness or for continuation, let us administer it at all times at the beginning of the month and at the end of it, and in the middle of it and at any time it is.

But they weaken the whole body and cause fainting in many men.
And the healing power of the vents is proper to the vents of the two legs.
And the quality of the position of the vents is that you place the vent at the beginning, and suck it with a gentle suction, and do not prolong the position of the vents, but pause them lightly, and move them carefully, and that the humors come to the place by gradual arrival. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the place already reddened and inflated and that the flow of blood appears.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and then consider in the disposition of the body.
Because that man who has tender flesh of rare pores, you should scarify him once and no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And command the ventor to amplify the scarification and prolong it a little, and temper the suction with ease.
And if in the blood there is thickness, then you should scarify twice, because in the first scarification the way of subtilisation to the blood and its liquidity is opened. But in the second, the acquired scrutiny of the extraction of the thick blood.
And if the blood is very thick, then the third scarification should be made so that the last is obtained.
And finally, when we want to extract the blood little by little or a little, one scarification is enough for us.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we scarify with many scarifications; and if we knew that the blood was thick, then we should scarify with a prolonged scarification.
And the term of scarification in which is the depth of the incision only.
And if that one whose incision is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, then the places of the vents should be anointed more gently, wider, and more resolved; if it is in summer, as it is with olive oil of aloe or violet oil, or almond oil, or olive oil of quince; and if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or of Saint John's wort, and to those similar.

And the one whose blood is of much humidity, washes his vents with vinegar, or with myrtle and sumac water and similar to those.
And of the one whose superfluities are thick, wash his vents with old vinegar, or with water of hyssop, which is marjoram, or of a decoction of fennel, or of chamomile, and to those similar.
And the vent should be guarded before bathing; bathing should be administered near the bath for an hour or two, and he should sleep near the vent.

Oh, son, know that these foolish doctors and the common folk boast about this chapter, and those who never at any time looked at the books of the Ancients nor read even one letter from them.

First Chapter.
Summary and collections from the being of fractures of bones, from those whose preamble is necessary before we begin the remembrance of broken and diverse members, of each one. Chapter Second: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and their figures are diverse, and the causes are distinct, because of fractures some is made by percussion of a blunt object, and the percussion stops all the bone inside the skull, which is left the bone, as if it were done in the shaft, and by this kind this species is named ascial; or the blunt object incides some part of the bone, and incides the flesh of it and does not pass the incision inside the back of it; and this species of fracture is named elevation absolute.
And the plague of these two fractures or is great or little, and of fracture that is compression or attrition; and cause of it is percussion with a strong blow or falling on stone, and their similitudes.
And this fracture or is penetrating that already approaches to the periosteum which is left the bone; or is in the face of the bone, and the plague of these two fractures or is great or little.
And of fracture is that which is made in the bone, in the subtlety of the small hair, and for that this species is named capillary fracture.
And is fracture that is made either by falling or by percussion of stone and its similitudes, that makes enter some lamina of the bone to the parts inside, and is made in the place concavity as it happens to the  payroles of ram when it happens to their percussion and is part of their entrance to the parts inside.
And most often this is done in the bodies of moist bones, as if it were the heads of infants.
And to all these species of fractures, are put weights as if they were stars separated. And I will come with remembrance of all that in its place.
And we shall know all these species of fracture by detection upon those and by inquiry of them with touches and examination of corrupted flesh that is upon them.
And a special capillary is known by this that it is discovered over the bone, and it is cleansed, and linen is laid on it with a bandage, because the fracture appears black.
In the cure of a fracture, at the beginning consider the accidents of the patient, and if you see by the accidents that signify by manifest signification of pain, such as vomiting of bile, and extension, and loss of understanding, and abscess of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and sweating of the joints and redness of those, and resemblances to them of the accidents in the major dispositions, then it is not to be healed.
And if you see accidents not current, then you hope for health, then you begin the cure of it.
The reason for this is that if it has come to you the afflicted at the beginning as it is afflicted and this is in the days of winter, then you must study in the placement of the bone before the 14th day according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then you must bind the bone before the 7th day, because that which is left of the bone be corrupted by the panniculus, and that the accidents that we have recounted may occur.
And if the fracture has already arrived at the panniculus covering the brain, and it is with small pieces and attrition, then you must insert the minute part and rub it, according to as I shall recount to you.
And it is that you raise the head of the afflicted patient, and uncover over the bone according to custom and manner to you possible according to the figure of the plague, and that it be light upon the patient.
And if accidents happen to you, you must detect the bone, flows of blood or abscess caut, then you oppose it with what you must, and it is that you fill the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and make the patient rest until the abscess is settled, and you are sure of the flow of blood; and then you begin in the circuit of the bone and in the ablation of it.
And this is according to one of two ways of operation.
And the one way is that you insert the bone with a straight incisor, and this is the form of it: And then administer another incisor this, a little wider than that; and this is the form of it:
And then administer more the incisor wider than the second.
And you must have you a number of different incisors, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are at the end of the sharpness of their extremities, and are of Indian iron or steel.
And use of them with facility in percussion upon the incisor so that it does not strike the head and damage it.
And if the bone is strong, hard, then you must perforate in the circuit of it before you administer the incisors with the drills, which are called non-deep drills.
And they are not called thus but because those do not stop the term of the bone of the test to that which is near it, for because the drill is round extremity or margin upon that which is left the head of it acute resembling a margin or a small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these drills a great number of which as many as convenient to the thickness of each bone,  within that it be to you present in all tests of the brain the extremity of which be acute, the quantity in length and brevity be according to the quantity of thickness of this test.
And this is the form of three species of drills: The quality of perforating in the circuit of the broken bone is that you pause the drill upon the bone and revolve that with your hands until you know that the bone is already penetrated; and then change the drill to another location, and pause that which is between all the tract the quantity of the thickness of the ray or around that.
And then insert with incisors that which is between each tract of the bone, and make that as subtle as you can upon that in the end of lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, or with your hand, or with some other cause for some instruments which you will have prepared to that, as such as tongs and subtle forceps.
And you must guard with all caution that the incisor does not touch any cause of the cephalic.
And therefore, as you will have circled the bone perforating, and it is separated from the cephalic if it is continued with it, and you will have that extracted, you must raise and smooth that in the roughness of it of that which is in the bone with other instruments resembling an incisor, but however you must have it more thin and more subtle than the others incisors.
And if any pieces of the small bones and splinters remain, then take that with facility with that which you must of the instruments; and then cure the wound with liniments and ointments which we have said.
And the other way of operation is lighter, far from pain, which says Gualia and the lauzec for great lausor; and this is the sermon of it: Necessary is to you in the beginning that you begin to discover the part of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, until when you will have discovered that part and you will have placed below it the extremity of this incisor lent and light of which this is the form:
And it is that the lenticular part is long and does not cut any cause, and the acute part is of it in two parts forward in length, as sometimes; that the lenticular part is adjusted to the skull with the face of it inciting acute in the bone.
And then strike upon the incision with a small mallet until all the bone is cut with ease as it is revolved, and you are sure of the periosteum that no accident happens in it in any manner, and if the physician were the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he were sleeping.
Now if any cause is annexed to the bone of the periosteum in some places of the bone, cut that of it with the extremity of the lenticular incision, and deliver that of it with ease, because it is delivered of it its detriment and its pain.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the periosteum, and that which is absciss or is stayed of the bone is the face of it, and remains in it roughness and subtle satagias, then when you remove that roughness and eradicate those satagias with subtle razors by which you have prepared the number, of diverse quantities so that it is possible to you administer of those in all places the most convenient of them and the most right of them according to what in which you are losing your operation that broken bone and its figure.
And then when you administer in your ratio with which razors the bone at the beginning the most broad of those razors, and then use it near the most subtle of it, and do not cease to do so according to continuity until you arrive at administering the most thick and the most subtle of all of them.
But in any of the other fractures small capillaries and subtle fractures, then when you administer care according to what you are losing to the rectification of it.
And it is the cause that it is not ammended upon all of those who have studied in that art. And stick upon that which we have written have shown in great fractures.
And if the periosteum is cut when you eradicate the bone, then when you take cloth of it according to the quantity of the plague, and submerge that in rose oil, and place that over the orifice of the plague; and then take another cloth folded or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place that over the first cloth and do that as lightly as you can over that, that you do not weigh down the skull; and then administer over that bandage wide, and do not tighten that except for the quantity that the plague has only.
And leave that with this care for a day or for two until you are sure of the abscess caut; and then untie that and then administer medicines with which is superfluous siccitat, as such as root of linen and flour of herbs, and powder of incense and of aristolochia, and of similar to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that be pulverized over the plague, as such as are dried; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which is that it abstergesca and do not bite.
And keep yourself according to your virtue that the plague in your cure be clean; and not be in it surdicia of enguent nor other; nor do you allow to add virulence in it; because when it is adjusted upon the periosteum of the brain, it corrupts that and poyreys that, and accidies upon the patient according to that malice malignant.
And accidies in the skull of the head, as from it is modified the bone and mostly when it is less cared for, and it is black in the surface of it; and therefore consider then if accidies to the patient the accidents that we have said, know that it is parnicios ses tot dopte.
And if it does not accidies black but from the medicine that is placed over it, and it is in virtue of that medicine that make that blackness, then when you take of honey one part and of rose oil parts iii., and in those line you a cloth, and then place that over the skull; and then cure that with the species of cure that convenes until it be sanat.
Chapter .iii.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that it is not broken of the nose but two parts together more sovereign, or one of those some times, for so in it you have two bones, for the part left is cartilaginous, and it is not broken and does not accidies to it but attrition and tortuosity and sinuosity.
But if it is broken one of the two parts of it, then when you introduce the said little one into its nose, and that you equal that fracture inside with your said index, and the said pus outside, until it returns the nose according to its natural figure. And be done that by you with ease, and guard yourself that no cause by that your operation upon the patient pain.
And if the fracture is in the most sovereign part of the nose, and the said one does not reach to that, then when it be equal with the extremity of the ray in which it be little thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do the semblance of that.
And covet yourself with the restoration of it in the first day of the fracture, if it is possible; and if not, then after the vii or x of the days of the repose of the abscess caut.
And then, inside the extremity of the nose, insert a piece of cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or put two splints, if the fracture is in two parts, and make the splint thick enough to fill the hole of the nose.
And some of the old restorers said that the splints should be wrapped in leather and changed every day, but I haven't seen it; rather, I believe that the cloth should be wrapped in a mixture of albumen and powdered honey; and then leave it in place until the bone is set and the cartilage has hardened.
And sometimes, instead of a splint, it is inserted into the nose, a piece of cloth wrapped in lion's skin; and this is used when the nasal fracture is very severe and the patient is not prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do this, and if you want, do splints.
And if another time an abscess occurs, cauterize it, and apply a plaster to the nose with cerot or with a cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if an abscess does not occur, then you should plaster it with sesame flour and powdered quince seeds mixed with albumen; and then place a piece of lion's skin on top, and do not touch the nose for any reason.
But if the nasal bones are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then you should cut over them, and extract them with instruments suitable for that purpose; and then close the incision, and treat it with what consolidates and heals, and with appropriate plasters.
And if a plague occurs in the nose, then you should treat it with splints and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is broken.
When the jaw is broken and the fracture of it is not accompanied by a plague, then look, if the fracture is only outward and not broken in two and curved at the parts inside, then its knowledge is lighter.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then you introduce your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then introduce the index finger of your right hand; and push with that the inward flexibility of the fracture with ease to the outer parts, with your other hand from the outer part of the bone; and so decently equalize them.
And if the jaw fracture is already separated into two parts, then you should administer extension of both parts according to rectitude as much as possible for the equalization of it.
And if there is a concussion of teeth to disruption, then strengthen those of which you hope will remain with a thread of gold or silver or lead; and then place a cerot on the broken jaw, and place a piece of doubled cloth on top, and place a large, well-made piece of felt or a piece of sole leather equal in length to the jaw; and then apply it as you are equipped with the bandage of it and as you deem the application, until it is not destroyed.
And command the patient to use rest and tranquility, and place his food in a soft, lion's broth.
And if you estimate that the figure has already been altered in any way, then you take it off on the third day; and then rectify what has already been altered of it and plaster it with powdered honey, with albumen, or with sesame flour, because you will have removed the cerot from it, and place wool on top of the plaster.
And thus, when it stands attached and adjusted, that plaster on it, and the disposition of the bone is not altered, leave it, that you do not remove it until it is healed and strengthens the fracture; because many times this fracture is straight in three weeks.
And if another time an abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in the sedation of it until it is healed, and the abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a plague, then consider if it is a piece of the bone separated or splinters, then you should be subtle that you remove those splinters with what you deem appropriate in the removal of them from the instruments.
And if the orifice of the plague is narrow, then you should enlarge it with the fleubotomist according to the amount of your necessity.
And when you will have removed those splinters, and of those no sir remaugut remains, then you close the orifice of the plague if it is wide; and if not, place on it one of the plasters that you deem appropriate and heal it, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Of the restoration of the clavicle when it is broken.
Many times the clavicle is broken at the part in front of the muscle, and that fracture is made in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, or separated, or separated into two parts without causing splinters; and it is easy to restore it; or it is so that splinters occur in it, and it is difficult to restore it; or it is so that the fracture is with a plague.
And the operation in that, when the fracture is with a plague, is that you make two ministers present, so that one holds the adjutori and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the neck with that part.
And then, with your hands, close the fracture, ensuring that the shape of it is as it should be, and that there is little prominence or concavity.
And if you need more extension, then cover the patient's limb with a pad of cloth or wool, and make its size according to your need; and extend and elevate the shoulder, and compress the pad with your hand while you close the fracture as it should be.
And if you cannot pull the shoulder limb outwards, because it has reached the armpit, then cover the patient's head, and place a cushion of medium size on the muscle of the shoulder.
And the minister presses the muscle of the shoulder towards the armpit until the bone of the shoulder is elevated as it is in the armpit, and then you rectify the fracture and close it with your hands.
But if you feel pains in the shoulder and they are bad, then cover that area and remove those pains with ease.
And if it is held in the bone, then you make an incision of it with one of the scissors which you have prepared for this, because you had placed below the shoulder the instrument that serves the chisel; and it is an instrument of wood or iron of which this is the form:
And it is similar to a chisel of which this is the concavity; and its width is according to what you need of it, according to the size of the bone or the smallness of it.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible to you in the operation; that it has two extremities thus as well, and one extremity is wide and the other narrow.
And if the disruption of the plaque which you have incised when you have extracted the pain from the bone is wide, and there is sureness of pus cauter, then you adjust the two lips of the plaque with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear pus cauter, then you fill the plaque with cloths and powders according to the quantity of the cut of the plaque.
And if pus cauter happens, then put a cloth soaked in vinegar and olive oil and wine, and place it over it.
And all, that the plate is not bad over the broken bone; and so that you see the patient every day, that when the ligament is softened or you see the plate already remaugued, then you rectify that and strengthen the binding.
And then place the sleeping of the patient on the neck of it, and place below the limb of it during the night a small cushion; so that with this it is elevated the helper of it from the side of it, and therefore is elevated the fracture of the shoulder to elevation of the muscle; or be the arm of it tied to the neck of it.
And do not untie the binding unless you happen in the place precisely or the abscess, within twelve days.
And then renew the plaster if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the stricture and restore that and let it until it is restored and coagulated the fracture of the shoulder.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the measure in twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in less time than that.
Chapter VI.
Of the Restoration of a Fracture of the Shoulder.
The spatula tart is broken in the place lateral of it and not broken of it but the edges.
And therefore when it is broken the place of it - and it is broken in the middle place of it - and it is not known except by touch, then according to what is the figure of the fracture forces you the preparation of it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every manner of ingenuity which is to you possible.
And then place on the place powder of wheat with egg white and linen cloth; and then place powdered cloth doubled; and then place over it a plate of fine wood according to the quantity of the spatula whole or more a little.
But if below the plate is concave in some of the places of the spatula, then you close that concavity with linen cloth, and then raise the plate according to equality.
And then strengthen above with a long strip with constriction decent and with firmness of the plate until it is not remaugued from its place.
And consider the ligament on each day; and every time it is softened, tie another one and tighten it and equal the star if it is moved from its place.
And place the patient on their back.
And the spatula is restored in 20 days or in .25.
And therefore when this number is completed, then you dissolve the ligament, and that you know for sure that that is of the bones of which the beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if you proceed from the place of the bone of the foot and place the bandage, then you split it on top, and remove it, and do as we have said in the fracture of the clavicle of the saddle, cauterize if it happens here.
Chapter .vii.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot sometimes is broken in the middle part of it and that is hard; but the extremities of it must be that they are healed and comminuted.
And of the accidents of it when it is broken the middle of it is that it declines to the parts of it and happens to it great pain, and difficulty in the hale, and coughs, and fort leu and vomiting blood; and it is declared in it the cavity in the broken bone and is not hidden to the feeling.
And the restoration of it is that it be raised the patient on their back, and place between the thighs of it a cushion; and then press on the muscles of it, and adjust the back of it with the hands of two sides, and subtiliate thee in the operation of it and in the equation according to all manner that is ad this possible and that convenes ad this, until that returns the figure of the bone according to that cove.
And then place on it plaster and lint and place above the star of table subtle of gauze or of caligi and of their similars in levitat, because that you envelop that in cloth; and then subtiliate thee in lian that over the broken bone that it not be harmful; and go with ligation according to roundness to the back sometimes, and tighten that with stricture decent; and then consider the ligation in every hour, and when it is softened tighten that.

Chapter .viii.
Of the restoration of the back when it is broken.
Know that fracture of the back does not catch but in places gross that follow the back; and of the extremities of them of the part in front does not happen to those contricio but because that they are cartilaginous. And cognition of it is not hidden to the feeling, therefore the inquiry of it with the fingers.
