Chapter IX.
Of the restoration of fractures of the vertebra of the back and neck.
Sometimes there is a fracture in the bones of the neck, and then there is a separation, and similarly in the vertebrae of the back. And if this happens to someone and you want to know if it will heal or not, consider this: if the times their hands are already softened and stunned and dead and cannot move them or extend them or tighten them and when you constrain them with your fingers or prick them with a needle and they feel no pain in them, then you know that it is not healing, and in the worst of dispositions it is mortal.
And if they move and feel pain in them from constriction with the fingers and puncture, then you know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with cure.
And if you see in the vertebrae of the neck the same thing and you want to know if it will heal or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already softened, it happens to those that is happening in the hands, and when you put your hand on their back, there is a feeling of airiness and expulsion of their will, and sometimes he lies on his back and wants to urinate and cannot, then you know that it is mortal, and therefore do not work in the cure of him.
But if you do not see any cause of those things, the cause is better and less severe.
And the cure of him is that you perforate at the seat of the abscess with a cautery that you place on the fractured vertebra with olive oil or with boiled wool; place that on it several times a day until the abscess is cauterized.
And then place one of the comforting and existing plasters, and strengthen it on that with a bandage; and command the patient to use rest and tranquility and that he not sleep on the part with which he feels pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that the separation of the bone is severe or any other cause that is already separated from it, then cover that which is separated on the bone and that you hold that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then cure that with some ointment of the ingredients, until it is healed.
But if the tailbone is broken, and it is the end of the coccyx, then cover that which is separated on the bone and that you hold that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then cure that with some ointment of the ingredients, until it is healed.
And if you feel splinters broken in it, then cover that which is separated on the bone and that you hold that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound, with sutures if it is large; and then cure that with some ointment of the ingredients, until it is healed.
Chapter X.
Of the restoration of the shoulder.
The bones of the shoulders are sometimes broken, and if they break, then the fracture of them is nothing but a communication at the extremities of them and they are incised in the length and incline to the sides; and it happens to the patient pain in the place and puncture, and the shoulder is stunned when it is fractured.
And the restoration of it is that you extend your hand over it so that it is over the fracture as is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is at the extremity of it, then you adjust that according to how it is situated by equation adjust until it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the long or that which already inclines to the sides, then you make the patient lie on their belly until it is prepared for you the restoration of that fracture. And therefore when you have adjusted it, place on it plaster, and then place on that a splint of wood or of bone, and strengthen by stricture over which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the splint.
And adjust the concavity with that which fills it so that that presses the stricture according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if there happens an abscess cauterize, then you cease from extension of it and its restoration until it is cauterized the abscess, and place on it that which seizes it according to what is preceded; and then return to the restoration of it, and strengthen it thus as it is covered.
And if splinters happen in the bone or they are comminuted, then you take them out.
Chapter XI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
Arm is that which is between the armpit to the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then the restoration of it is in one of two ways, of which is the one that you take wood plank of moderate thickness according to this form:
And have loops at two extremities two bandages, and then it is suspended from the elevated place.
And follow the patient on a chair; and then throw out his broken arm onto the wood until it reaches the tip of it in the middle place of which is annexed the curvature of the wood.
And then, if something heavy is placed upon it, or the minister extends it to the sides, then the physician, with his hands, joins the fracture together, while the fracture returns as it should.
And the other way is that the patient be laid on his neck and the hand on his neck is held; and he commands two ministers that one of them holds that which is above the fracture with his hands; and the other holds the patient's legs; and each of them extends to his side.
And if you want the extension to be more forceful, then you strengthen upon the fracture with one hand and upon that with another hand, and each of the two ministers extends to his two sides.
And therefore, if the fracture is near the extremity of the muscle, then it should be placed half of the ligament on the  citillic and the other behind the fracture of the thigh.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the thigh, then it should be placed the ligament on that location and on that thigh.
Engage that with ease and work, and as soon as it is engaged according to what is proper and applied with good application, then strengthen that if it does not accidentally cause a boil caut.
But if an abscess occurs, then loosen the constriction within the seventh day, and place on it wool cloth soaked in vinegar and olive oil until the abscess rests, and then you strengthen that.
And the way of strengthening that is that you place a plaster over the fracture, and then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm over that support, and place your hand open over their muscle.
And place the cloth and strengthen over the support the arm so that the arm is in a place of stars, if you do not prohibit from that any cause of the broken bone.
And then use of stars.
And it is that you place over the fracture that star which is more wide and more strong of the other stars; and place between each star the width of the finger, and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers of every part.
And then strengthen over the stars with the constriction which I have said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your constriction over the location of the fracture be more tight. And as much as it is lengthened the fracture be the constriction less.
And therefore, if you see the situation of the stars and the constriction as we have said in the hour in which you restore the limb to its position, then you do.
And if you fear an abscess, then you loosen the constriction and the stars within the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the ligament in all parts for three days so that nothing happens in the place proximate or inflammation; or is prohibited the food to arrive at the limb by disposition of superfluity of constriction; and therefore rectify all that according to what we have said.
But if you are on confidence that nothing will happen from that, then do not untie the ligature until after three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep on his back, and his hands be on his stomach.
And place on the support a cushion full of wool well engaged, and consider at every hour of the night or of the day that the figure of the broken limb be not loosened nor dissolved, or that the ligament be softened, therefore rectify that all with your virtue.
And place the patient's food according to the order which we have before said, in such a way that the food be subtle at the beginning until it is begun to strengthen; and then it is that you increase the food of him.
Because of custom of the support and of the bed of him is that they be strengthened in forty days; and then it is that they should be untied and be administered baths and be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is laid irritated, then you do not untie from that the ligament and the stars within the days or within two months.
Chapter .xii.
Of the Restoration of a Fracture of the Arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two forearms; of which one is small, and that is the one that lies the little finger, and the other is large and that is the one which is placed behind the little finger, from the part more behind.
And often the major forearm is broken alone, or the small one, and often they are broken together.
And when the small forearm is broken above, then the restoration of it is lighter, and the healing of it is lighter.
And when the forearm of it below the fracture of it is bad, and the healing of it is difficult.
And worse of that is when the two bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone which is broken is the small forearm above, then it is convenient to the physician, that the restoration of it, that place small extension with ease until that be engaged.
But if the large forearm is that which is broken, then it is that you place extension more vehement.
And if the two forearms are together broken, then it is that you place the extension more strongly.
And while you are holding the figure of your hand, you must restore it and extend it over a cushion, and the wrist of your hand should be at the top, more than all the others, and the earlobe should be lower than the others, and the patient should be lying on a fallen cradle.
And there should be a cushion in front of him in the height or upright position of him, so that the patient does not move.
And then the minister extends the arm from the back side with his hand or ligament, and another minister extends upwards seemingly.
And then the doctor aligns the bones while reducing it according to the best figure that he can.
And if in the fracture of the bones there are splinters, then you must pierce to reduce all the pieces in their place with your virtue.
And if there appears a splinter separated that pierces the tendon and it is not in your hope for the restoration of it, then you must place it on top and hold it according to the manner which we have said above.
But if the fracture is with a plate, I have already made a singular chapter about this, and therefore take the cure of this.
And if an abscess happens at the beginning of your restoration, then you line a cloth with cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and it should be of medium between thickness and fineness, and strengthen the cloth over it with a subtle structure until the abscess is reduced; and then remove the cerate and place a plaster prepared of powdered mold with the clear of egg, and then place stars; and let the star be the one which is placed over the fracture more wide and more strong.
And know that the number of the stars of the arm is six in the major disposisitions, or whether the fracture is in one focus or in two foci together.
And then place your structure over the place of the fracture more strong and more vehement, and when more it is situated at the top parts of the fracture or at the lower parts of it, place the structure a little more soft according to that it is preceded the remembrance of it in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are enveloped over the fracture should be soft and moist cloths, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is strengthened should be of the proper medium between subtle and coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the limb and the ligament after a few days. And if there happens any cause for which rectification is necessary, as prudently as the one that happens to the limb, and then you must immerse the limb in warm water until that prudence ceases.
And let the limb not be constrained until it rests, and then return the structure.
And if the structure is already softened and the bone is already reduced and seems like that, then rectify everything with your virtue.
And consider more if the nourishment is prohibited to reach the limb by superfluity of structure, because then you must soften that one a little and let it for a few days until it runs to that the nourishment, and then strengthen it.
But if the patient does not happen any of those causes that we have said, then he should not be dislocated for about 20 days or so.
And then let the arm of the patient be suspended by its virtue of movement agitation, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or 32 days, or strong it is restored in 28 days, and all this is according to the disposition of the complexities and the dispositions of the virtue.
Chapter .xiii.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the finger of the hand and of the fingers.
Know that to the finger of the hand and to the joints of the fingers, it often happens that fracture, and it does not happen to them but attrition many times.

And then the minister extends the broken bones and the doctor aligns them while they are conjoined with good conjoined.
And then you must place plaster and cotton wool, if there is no abscess; and then place stars above according to the quantity of the place, and you have already enveloped that in cloth of it.
But if the fracture is in the parts of the fingers of the palm, and then make a semblance of a sphere of cloth, and command the patient that he strengthen that with his fractured palm; then it should be strengthened with long cloth.
And let the star be of leather with cloth in which it is elevated, so that the leather is applied to the palm; and strengthen that according to what is necessary.
But if the fracture is at the outside parts, then you must place above a star and another star from the lower part in the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then place the structure, as the hand is revolved to the wrist between the fingers with ligaments.
And if a fracture happens to one of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the finger laid, it should be aligned as necessary and then strengthened with the palm, and if you want, place a small straight star to it so that the fracture is elevated and not damaged.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as in the middle finger, or in the index, or in the ear, or in the ring finger, then it should be bandaged and tied with the bandage that is the same as that which we have said in the hand; or they should all be tied according to continuity, and this is better; or rest on that small splint, as we have said in the hand.
And consider, in your restoration, and in that place, the disposition of cautery abscess and oppose — in your operation — to what happens when any cause of those things occurs, according to what is narrated about it.
Chapter XIV.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the thigh.
The bone of the thigh, when broken, is felt and declared according to how it is converted to the front and the superior part.
And the restoration of it is done in such a way that the ligament is tightened over the fracture and another ligament is left below the fracture, and the patient is prone on his belly and face.
And then the minister extends all the ligament to its part according to the quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is near the root of the thigh, then the ligament is pulled down to the part of the pubic bone, so that the extension is there, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the pubic bone, then the ligament is pulled down to the pubic bone, and the extension is to the parts below.
And the physician aligns the bone with both its hands until it is reduced according to the natural appearance and the bone is joined by a good connection; then cover it with plaster and a splint, unless an abscess occurs in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then you leave it for a few days until the abscess cauterizes, and then return to your cure.
The splint of it should be made like this: cover the fracture with a hard bandage, and fold it two or three times, and remove the superfluity from it; and then wrap the leg until it reaches the thigh and the root of the thigh.
And introduce a long splint between the thigh and the leg from the pubic bone to the parts below, and the ends of the splint from the superior part of both parts, and then roll over the leg and thigh what superfluity of the bandage.
And then place on the thigh in the place of that fracture splints, and place one splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the splint is coagulated.
And then begin with a strong splint in the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings, and as much as you move away with the ligament from the place of the fracture, your splint should be smaller and lighter and softer.
And then extend both ends of the splint to the parts below until it reaches the ankle of the foot, and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the ligament is not removed from its place.
And then leave the splint on it, so that it does not occur to the limb corrosion or cauterized abscess or inflammation and similar things; and if any cause of those things occurs, then cut it from it, and rectify what occurs again, according to what we have said many times.
But if in the bone is the weight of the bone which puts, then cover it according to what is possible to you; and if not, then put on it and remove that, and cure with what precedes the recollection until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with other things that are adjusted to it, like we have said in the arm and the helper.
But however, in the restoration of it in this manner, do not occur to the patient lameness; and if it is restored alone, then apply something to the other leg, then it is necessary that he limp on the one that is always.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in 40 days or a few more or a few less, and all this is according to the diversity of the complexities and of the other dispositions.
Chapter XV.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bone of the pubic bone.
Know that a fracture of the bone of the pubic bone is difficult, but it often occurs with a lot of pain.
But if a fracture occurs, then it is not more than a fissure or a crack in its parts, and this is with a bandage or a few bandages, and over this all is placed with the feeling.
And the restoration of it is in such a way that what is broken of its parts is aligned with the fingers until it is joined and connected according to what is possible by equation and facility and fabrication.

And consider all the dispositions which we have recommended in the other fractures, as well as abscess cauterization and similar things, so that it opposes to all accidents to what is convenient to it until it is healed.
Chapter XVI.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones, of which one is thick, and is called by the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and is called the fibula.
And it happens in some species of fracture that it happens to both bones of the leg, and therefore the restoration of it is done as the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation of it is one.
And if two bones are broken together, the leg is converted to all parts.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is converted to the parts in front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, it stands that way, and it is of those causes that are not to your liking.
And then administer extension and equation and ligation of the splints according to that same manner.
However, if the fracture of the leg is recent and has many stages, that the extension be less and lighter, and alleviate with your virtue.

And already it is administered a splint of wood according to the length of the leg; and be placed in it the leg so that it conserve that of movement.

Chapter XVII.

Chapter .xx.
Of the cure of a nodule that happens in the trace of any fracture.
Many times happens this nodule in the trace of healing of a fracture, and mostly that it is near the joints, and it is disfigured by it the figure of the limb; and very soon prohibits the limb of its natural operation.

Chapter .xxi.

Chapter XXIII.
Of the speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of its place, so that it is prohibited from movement, and the figure of the limb is distorted, and it causes great pains and injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when an accident happens to a dislocation, then it should be immediately reduced, and that reduction should not be delayed, because if it is delayed, the place is suppurated and very sore, and with water it is made difficult the reduction of the dislocation.
And for that reason, it should not be bad or stretched at the time of suppuration, because many times it brings on spasms and intense pains.
But when this happens, then it should be that you take care of the patient's  flambotomy, and then let it rest until the suppuration rests; and then the limb should be bandaged with warm water and oil, and then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be cured with that which the remembrance will come back to its place.
And you have already ordered the chapters of dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the part of the body most sovereign to the part most below.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of dislocation of the lower jaw.
So the two jaws are dislocated if not tart; and the dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: that they are remaugged from their places by little movement, and they are softened a little; or they are dislocated by entire dislocation and complicated, until they are softened at the junction where the saliva of the patient flows and that it cannot hold that nor can it close its mouth, and the tongue of it is enveloped in its word.
But when the dislocation of it is little, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself with little cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complicated, then it should be administered reduction of it lightly, and not be delayed at all.
And it is that the minister has the head of the patient, and the doctor inserts his two fingers of his hand into the root of the dislocation of the lower jaw from inside his mouth, if the dislocation is of one part; or that he inserts his two fingers together, if the dislocation is of two parts, and the other said of the others of him are outside with which he pulls; and commands the patient to cease his jaws and absolves those to go from any part; and the doctor pulls the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.

Chapter .xxvi.

Chapter XXVII.

Chapter XXVIII.
Of the cure of dislocation of the shoulder, which is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
Shoulder dislocation sometimes occurs, and the reduction of the dislocation of it is easier, and the opposite is of the other joints, but however, as long as there is speed in the reduction of it in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place is suppurated or that happens in it suppuration caut.
And the reduction of the dislocation of it is that you put the shoulder joint of the patient on a table and the minister extends the arm of that one, and the physician puts his hand or his palm on the prominence of the joint, and pushes it until it returns to its place.
But however, as long as it is considered if the palm is already put on the belly of the hand, then the patient puts the back of his hand on a table, despite the extension and the reduction; and if the dislocation is previous, then the position of the hand of that one intrinsically on the table, so that the hand of the physician lies on that prominence of the joint.
And therefore, if it returns quickly, it is good; and if not, then push it with a poultice, letting the suppuration and leave it; and do not repeat this, because if not, do not suffer nor can you over the reduction of it more than passing to it days.
But however, the joint remains according to its tortuosity; and do not at night to the patient any cause, unless the hand is softened and cannot push any cause; then you know that the nerves are cut or torn, and then it is not generated in it unless it is pushed with a cautery near the place of it and strongly do you confer any cause.
Therefore, when the shoulder is returned, then put on it the poultice which you have recommended, and let it be left and pushed for 5 days; and then remove it and let it be left and the hand of it be exercised.
But if the movement of it is stayed and happens in it any gravity, then gently that with warm water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Chapter XXIX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the said.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated in all parts.
And when it is dislocated some finger of those at the back of the palm or on the belly of it, then stretch the finger and push the dislocation of it with your thumbs, until it returns.
And then support the head of the finger, and suspend that of the part which is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until straight it is aligned in that day.
And when it is night, tie that according to that narration, and do not cease to untie that in the day and exercise with movement, and tie that at night; and do this for several days until it is straight.
And do seeming with this, if it is dislocated on the belly of the hand, and tie that to that part and do with this your first operation, until it is healed.
And similarly do with this when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Chapter XXX.
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When it happens to any of the vertebrae of the back or of the neck a complicated dislocation, and so removed motz vertebrae from their places, then in that there is no cure, because death takes hold of the patient.
And a sign of this is that the digestion of the patient is outside its will, and it cannot retain it; and many times are softened some limbs of it or both legs of it or both arms of it, or one of those; or is removed a vertebra from its place, because many times it is removed and many times is made the removal of those in four parts.
Therefore that is already removed to the parts behind and is named gelbozitat.
But the cure of it is that then consider if the gelbozitat happens of puerility, because then in that there is no cure nor of that is healing of all.
But in that which comes by accident or by percussion or seeming to those, already said the Ancients the manners of cure with sermons prolixs, of which the most returns with prophecy; but I already and shortened of that of which the little excuse of the too much of it, for the my proximity to the my better understanding exposition, with which I have here exposed and formatted ex instrument divers of that with which I show this and expose that.
And of those, it is not cured except that which occurs in the two properly with what I recommend.
And that the patient be extended on his back, as close to the wall as possible, and be placed under him a soft pillow so that the feet of him are not lifted.
And then a straight stick be placed, fixed in the ground at the extremity of the arm around his head, and another stick in front of his feet at the other extremity of the arm.
And the minister hold the stick, and not be fixed in the ditch; and another minister hold the other according to that form.
And then envelop over the head of the patient and below the limbs firmly; and extend the extremity of the limb to the stick which is opposite his head, and tie that in it; and then strengthen the other ligament over the hips of him the thighs, and opposite the legs; and then adjust all the ligaments and tie those in the other stick which is opposite the feet of him.
And then the minister carefully extends the ligament to the part of the stick, and not be removed the two sticks and the ligaments from their places in which they are fixed, but however not be fixed as we have said.
And the physician places his palms on the spine with strength while returning, or what places upon that a table, and then that adjusts upon the table his feet while returning.
But if it does not return by this form, then take a table of which the width or length is opposite of three arms, and carve in the wall of which we have said that is near the patient a place in which enters the extremity of the table. And then places the majority of the table upon the spine, the physician places his feet upon the other extremity, and tightens well while the spine is contracted and that it returns to its place.
And if you want, open with a turn which is revolved with the hand; and it is that you fix in the ground opposite the head of the patient in the back of the arm two sticks, the length of which is of an arm, and opposite his head in the back of the arm also two sticks; and be the length that is between each stick a palm, and already is made in each stick a hole in which runs the turn, and each one be moved fixed in the ground so that it not be moved.
And be also a round stick in which is the turn, and be revolved in the two holes of the two sticks.
And in the extremity of it is a hole in which is formed the stick, the length of which is a palm with what is revolved; and in the two other sticks is similar to that.
And then strengthen the ligaments that you have stretched in the feet of the patient in the hole which is opposite his head, and the ligaments that you have stretched in the thighs of him in the turn which is opposite the feet of him.
And then each turn and stay a minister that twists the turn with his hand, and the physician tightens the spine according to what we have said.
And as soon as the spine is returned, and the place is tightened, then cover that you pause plaster exsiccative with album of egg, and then tow; and then place upon the plaster a board of the table of which the width is three fingers or about those, and the length of it is the quantity that embraces the place of the hernia, and upon the part of the spine say.
And then be tied with the ligament which we have said, and use the patient of subtle nourishment while it is healing.
And if there remain any prominence in the place at the end of the healing, then cover that medicines be administered which soften and cleanse with administration of the table for long time, which we have recommended; and already is administered in that place a sheet of lead.
And some times there occur prominence in the back of the spines of the two, and it is cured by that dislocation; and it is that bone which already protrudes for that cover not be made presumption to it with this medicine, for very strong evil will it do to you to happen death.
Chapter .XXXI.
Of the cure of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle ought not to be dislocated only, and that which occurs to those that occurs to the other joints of small movement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated according to four manners, for it is dislocated to the front parts, or to the back parts, or to the front parts, or to the back parts.
And mostly it is dislocated to the outward parts, and few to the front parts or to the back parts.
And the sign of the dislocation of it to the outward parts is that, when you adjust the thigh of the healthy patient with the patient, it is longer and the thigh is more prominent than of the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his foot opposite the knee, and it is the place that the knee is inflamed of abscess caut, so that the head of the thigh has already arrived here.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation happens is in the outside parts, that the accidents of him are contrary to this accident.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation happens in the front parts is that he expands his leg according to compliance, but however, he does not double that pain that is in the thigh, and if it is pierced to go and cannot above that in the front parts; and the urine of him is retained and the groin of him is suppurated, and he must the ambulation of him on the crutch.
The sign of the one to whom dislocation happens in the back parts is that he does not expand the thigh, nor can he bend that in front which bends the groin, and the leg of him is shorter than the other, and the groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is that place.
But the reduction of the species of this dislocation is that you consider, therefore, if the dislocation is old and antique, that it is already anchored with that, then you administer reduction of it and it remains according to its disposition, then it is not in that cure of all; and therefore, no cove that in that be made presumption.
But in that one whose dislocation is recent, and it is one of the four species of dislocation, then you take hold of it so that you turn the joint and extend it to the front parts and the back parts, and move it to the right and left, for it returns strongly and does not need other cure.
But, if it does not return in this manner, then you must prepare a strong minister and extend the leg of him from the farthest part, or with his hands, or with a bandage which is tied over the leg below the thigh; and another minister extends the hand of him below so that he puts his hands below the armpits of him; and then tighten with a bandage on the root of the thigh, and hold another minister the extremity of the bandage.
And that extension of him or from the front part to the groin to the furcula, or from the back part to the part of the loins.
And that extension of all those in one go while the patient is elevated with his body from the ground and that remains hanging.
And this species of extension is common to all four.
And therefore, if the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to all species what I will say of the proper cure.
In the reduction of it proper, when it is dislocated, and the dislocation is in the front parts, it is necessary that the patient be laid on his side; and then place the bandages on the root of the thigh in that place which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is below the groin; and then extend the bandage from above the part of the groin to the highest part of the furcula.
And then another minister strongly presses the arms of him, and embrace the thick part of the thigh of the patient and extend to the front parts by vehement extension, for that returns to its place.
And this species is of the lightest cure with which this member is reduced than the others.
But if it is excused on you and you do not obey the entrance with this species of cure of all, then it is necessary that the two legs of the patient are tied together with a strong, light bandage, on both the crutches and both the thighs.
And the length of each of them by the quantity of four fingers, and the leg of the patient extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
And then the patient be suspended over the head of the pole, and that be in the middle; and the length of him from the ground the quantity of two arms.
And then command a strong young man to press down on the head of the thigh, and that be suspended with the patient another young man, and the other young man pressing the thigh with strength compels, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in the proper reduction, when the dislocation is in the front part, it is necessary that the patient lie on his back as we have recommended in the having flexibility; and be tightened the bandage on the thigh of the patient properly and on his feet.
And then two poles be placed, one at his feet, and the other at the head; and then be placed poles adjusted in the middle of the neck laid on which wet cloths be enveloped, so that the patient not be damaged for that the pole be between his thighs, for that the extension to the back parts not be pulled.
And then each minister extends his part, and the physician with his hand engulfs the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is in the front parts, then it is necessary that the leg be extended slightly, and it is according to this same narration about the neck; and the physician places the palm of his right hand on the groin of the patient, and then forces that with the other hand, and with that force places that extended to the back parts to the part of the thigh.
And if the dislocation is in the back, then the patient should not be stretched to the front, and it should be raised above the ground; it should be placed on a hard surface, just as it should be placed on a hard surface if the joint is dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about the placement of the joint on two widths; and it should be on its face and the ligaments should be stretched, according to what we have said, there is no harm.
And it should be administered compression with a board over the area where the joint is.
Therefore, as the reduction is complicated according to what is said — and the sign of the reduction of it is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend the patient's two legs, and when you see those equal and the patient extends his leg and expands that excuse, then you know that the limb according to what is said is returned — and then you adjust two cushions and place a poultice and extend with a bandage so that the joint is not damaged in any part; and adhere the patient to rest for three days or for four.
And then unwrap the ligaments and the poultice, and compare one leg to the other.
Then, if you see those equal in alignment, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed, and therefore let the patient go.
And if you see any cause of softening, then you return and plaster that, and extend that according to your first structure, and let it be in three days.
And then unwrap that, and make that delay in walking for some days, until it is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways, which is dislocated outwards, inwards, and forwards to the back; and forwards it is not dislocated.
And the sign of dislocation of it is that you command the patient to apply his leg to the thigh; then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of it is that you make the patient straight, so that he already extends his leg, if he can over that; adhere your two hands to the face of him, and place his foot between your feet; and then make adhere the knee of him to your palms, and keep those between your fingers on the knee of him with strength, and another minister extends his leg while the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction of it is that his leg adheres to the thigh with light no-labor.
And then plaster that and make adhere the leg to the thigh, and then tie those together at once with a bandage for three days or four, and then unwrap those.
And do not use the patient for walking for some days, until it is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments of which in the cure of the joint preceding narration.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle some times is sprained by slight motion, and some times is dislocated according to complement, and the dislocation of it is made either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of the dislocation of it is that you see the ankle inflated proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of the sprain of it is of slight reduction, and it is that it be extended with ease with the hands and be aligned while it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to complement, it is necessary that you make the patient straight, and have that one minister strong and valiant from the back of the two of that in the middle place of it.
And then you with your right hand the foot of the most sovereign part, and with your left hand of the part most below of the foot in the place of the ankle.
And then you pull the foot to you with your right hand, and with your left hand for two times, and then extend with your left; and push the heel of the foot with your right to the leg's work.
And do this two times according to what we have counted.
And then push the heel of the foot to the leg the third time, and you are at the ankle.
Therefore if it returns in one time or in two according to this manner, and if you see the foot aligned, it is good; and if not, then over it iterate the operation, for that returns.
But if to you is prohibited the reduction of it with that we have recounted, then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a piece of cloth on the ground firmly, and that it lies between the thighs of him, and you have already enveloped over him cloth so that it does not scratch the patient.
And then the minister of him holds his thighs, and another minister extends the leg with his hands or with ligaments that lie over the neck of the foot; and then extend that each minister against extension of his part, and the cloth be extant between the thighs of the patient retaining that, so that the body of him not be pulled to the front according to its extension.
And then the doctor grasps the dislocation with his hands, and another minister holds the healthy limb aside, because the dislocation returns with ease.
And therefore, when the dislocation returns and you declare the health of its reduction, then apply a plaster and bind that firmly with strong bandages, and tie the leg to the bed.
And be careful of the nerves which are behind on the heel, so that the bandage on that area is not too strong and damages it.
And leave that for two or three days; and if the bandage is softened, remove it.
And then unwrap it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from exercising for 40 days. But if he is forced to go before that time, it is not certain that the dislocation will not be dissolved and corrupted on him, and then that it will not receive healing.
And if an abscess occurs to him, then you must administer in the treatment of it what I have previously narrated in another place of healing and ointment, until it is healed.
Chapter .xxxiiii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toes.
Take care that the one who is dislocated be grasped with little extension of his work; and that which is not difficult, do it gently.
But if the dislocation is in some joints of the foot, then you must make the patient place his foot on a level surface of the ground or table, and be standing as if he were going.
And then you stand on your feet, and place your foot on what you feel of the joints, and then press that with your foot with force until it returns, and that you see it already aligned and does not appear in the place prominence.
And then place under the foot of him a board that supports the whole foot, which has two ends, and then bind that with a decent and firm structure for three days; and then unwrap it and make him rest from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and its return is assured.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the kinds of dislocations that are made with plague or with fracture or with each one together.
When any of those causes occurs, and you engage in the cure of it and in the restoration of it, many times death occurs.
And for this reason, you must not presume on the cure of it, but rather that one who is wise in the art, of long usage, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not rash nor presumptuous; and that he uses in the beginning of medicines that cause abscesses, only so much, and leave the patient to the divine judgment, but rather to him whom you hope for salvation from destruction, with the alleviation of the illness, and in which appears some hope. And therefore, strive as soon as possible to make the reduction of it at the beginning of the cause, before it develops into a dangerous abscess.
And therefore, if the limb returns as we want, then administer the regimen that causes a dangerous abscess and cures the plague with what is convenient for it of the dried herbs.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and occurs in the bone shattered and separated, then you must pierce the stem of them, and do in that what we have said in the simple ailments of this which is preceded in those places.
And guard and avoid according to your virtue that of the treatment in the ways of fear according to what has preceded my preaching to you; for this is better to you, you owe your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and more greetings to your true body those in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasis perfectly of which may God be the benefit. Amen.
